Here is the full analysis:\n\n---\n\n## Structured Analysis of 8 Papers\n\n---\n\n### 1. PMC5157199\n**File:** `/app/papers/PMC5157199.md`\n\n**Full Content Summary:**\nA multicenter, open-label Phase II trial evaluating selumetinib (AZD6244/ARRY-142886), an oral MEK-1/2 inhibitor, in 39 patients with iodine-131 refractory papillary thyroid carcinoma (IRPTC). The primary endpoint (objective response rate ≥20%) was not met — only 1 partial response (3%) was achieved. Stable disease occurred in 54% of evaluable patients, with a median progression-free survival (PFS) of 32 weeks. The paper found a non-significant trend toward improved PFS in BRAF V600E mutant tumors (33 vs. 11 weeks). The study assessed BRAF, NRAS, and HRAS mutations. Common adverse events included rash, fatigue, diarrhea, and peripheral edema. The paper concluded selumetinib should not be pursued in BRAF wild-type IRPTC and that future trials should stratify by BRAF V600E status.\n\n**Relevance to CYP2C19 (*1, *2, *3, *17):** Not relevant. No mention of CYP2C19, its alleles, or its metabolic phenotypes anywhere in the paper.\n\n**Drugs mentioned relating to CYP2C19:** None. The paper focuses on selumetinib (a MEK inhibitor), doxorubicin, axitinib, sorafenib, motesanib, and sunitinib — none of which are primary CYP2C19 substrates in this context.\n\n**Phenotypes/Conditions relating to CYP2C19:** None.\n\n---\n\n### 2. PMC5331771\n**File:** `/app/papers/PMC5331771.md`\n\n**Full Content Summary:**\nA cohort study of 336 Han Chinese patients treated with percutaneous coronary intervention (PCI) and followed for 1 year, investigating the effect of clopidogrel-related gene polymorphisms on platelet reactivity and cardiovascular clinical outcomes. Platelet reactivity was measured by the VerifyNow P2Y12 assay. CYP2C19 (*2, *3, *17), ABCB1, ITGB3, CYP2C9*3, CYP2B6*9, and P2Y12 alleles were genotyped. Key results: CYP2C19*2 and P2Y12 carriers had high on-treatment platelet reactivity (HTPR) and more adverse endpoints; CYP2C19*17 carriers showed improved (lower) platelet reactivity and fewer adverse endpoints. Carriers of CYP2C19*2 had more clinical endpoints (76.19% vs. 43.20%, P<0.001) and HTPR (194.25 vs. 151.38 PRU, P<0.001). CYP2C19*17 carriers had substantially lower platelet reactivity (97.17 vs. 169.08 PRU, P=0.003) and fewer endpoints. No cardiovascular deaths were observed in endpoint patients.\n\n**Relevance to CYP2C19 (*1, *2, *3, *17):** Directly and highly relevant. All three loss-of-function and gain-of-function variants are central study endpoints:\n- CYP2C19*2: associated with HTPR and more major adverse cardiovascular events (MACE)\n- CYP2C19*3: assessed as part of the loss-of-function panel\n- CYP2C19*17: associated with improved platelet reactivity and fewer clinical endpoints (gain-of-function)\n- CYP2C19*1 (wild type) is used implicitly as the reference group\n\n**Drugs mentioned relating to CYP2C19:**\n- Clopidogrel (primary subject of the study — a CYP2C19 prodrug requiring bioactivation)\n- Aspirin (used as part of dual antiplatelet therapy)\n\n**Phenotypes/Conditions relating to CYP2C19:**\n- High on-treatment platelet reactivity (HTPR) / clopidogrel resistance\n- Major adverse cardiovascular events (MACE) after PCI\n- Coronary artery disease\n- Poor metabolizer phenotype (CYP2C19*2 and *3 carriers)\n- Ultrarapid/enhanced metabolizer phenotype (CYP2C19*17 carriers)\n\n---\n\n### 3. PMC5533343\n**File:** `/app/papers/PMC5533343.md`\n\n**Full Content Summary:**\nA comprehensive pharmacogenetic meta-analysis of two large Phase III cardiovascular outcomes trials of darapladib — a lipoprotein-associated phospholipase A2 (Lp-PLA2) inhibitor — the STABILITY trial (stable coronary heart disease, n=15,828) and SOLID-TIMI 52 (acute coronary syndrome, n=13,026). Together, ~24,000 patients were genotyped using genome-wide arrays. The analysis investigated baseline Lp-PLA2 activity, efficacy endpoints (major coronary events, myocardial infarction), and tolerability (diarrhea, odor). Replicated six known loci (APOE, CELSR2, LPA, PLA2G7, LDLR, SCARB1) and identified three novel loci (TOMM5, FRMD5, LPL) influencing Lp-PLA2 activity. No variants associated with Lp-PLA2 activity at baseline were associated with drug response endpoints. Only one common variant showed a differential response to darapladib by treatment arm. The paper concluded that genetics did not identify meaningful responsive subgroups for darapladib. Darapladib failed its primary endpoints in both trials.\n\n**Relevance to CYP2C19 (*1, *2, *3, *17):** Not relevant. The paper includes ADME candidate gene analysis, and CYP enzymes are mentioned in the context of darapladib metabolism, but CYP2C19 specifically is not discussed, and no CYP2C19 alleles (*1, *2, *3, *17) are mentioned.\n\n**Drugs mentioned relating to CYP2C19:** None directly. Darapladib is the study drug. Statins (97% of patients) and metformin are mentioned as background medications, but no CYP2C19-specific drug interactions are discussed.\n\n**Phenotypes/Conditions relating to CYP2C19:** None.\n\n---\n\n### 4. PMC5903579\n**File:** `/app/papers/PMC5903579.md`\n\n**Full Content Summary:**\nA rationale and design paper for the International Clopidogrel Pharmacogenomics Consortium (ICPC), a multi-site (17 sites, 13 countries) consortium contributing individual patient data from 8,829 patients treated with clopidogrel. The goal is to perform candidate gene and genome-wide association studies (GWAS) to identify all genetic determinants of clopidogrel pharmacodynamics (platelet reactivity) and clinical response. Platelet function was assessed by VASP assay, Light Transmittance Aggregometry (LTA), and VerifyNow P2Y12 assay. A proof-of-principle analysis in 5,328 Caucasian patients confirmed that CYP2C19*2 was independently associated with higher on-treatment platelet reactivity (Beta=0.37; p=5.1×10−40). The paper provides a detailed review of CYP2C19 variants and their mechanisms, noting that CYP2C19*2 and *3 are loss-of-function (LoF) alleles reducing active metabolite levels, while CYP2C19*17 is a putative gain-of-function (GoF) allele. The paper also discusses ABCB1, CYP2B6, PON1, CES1, and B4GALT2 as other potential contributors.\n\n**Relevance to CYP2C19 (*1, *2, *3, *17):** Directly and highly relevant. CYP2C19 is the central gene of interest:\n- CYP2C19*2 (rs4244285): strongest known determinant of clopidogrel platelet reactivity, LoF allele — ~15% AF in Caucasians, ~29–35% in Asians; FDA boxed warning for *2 homozygotes (poor metabolizers)\n- CYP2C19*3 (rs4986893): LoF allele co-studied with *2\n- CYP2C19*17: putative GoF allele; linked genetically to *2 (not independently associated per Lewis et al.)\n- CYP2C19*1 implied as reference/wild-type\n\n**Drugs mentioned relating to CYP2C19:**\n- Clopidogrel (central drug — requires CYP2C19 bioactivation to active thiol metabolite for P2Y12 inhibition)\n- Prasugrel (compared, not affected by CYP2C19 polymorphisms)\n- Ticagrelor (compared, not affected by CYP2C19 polymorphisms)\n- Aspirin (co-administered)\n\n**Phenotypes/Conditions relating to CYP2C19:**\n- High on-treatment platelet reactivity (HTPR) / clopidogrel resistance\n- Poor metabolizer phenotype (CYP2C19*2 homozygotes)\n- Atherothrombotic events, stent thrombosis\n- Acute coronary syndrome (ACS)\n- Ischemic stroke\n- Post-PCI outcomes\n- Bleeding risk (CYP2C19*17 carriers)\n\n---\n\n### 5. PMC6786370\n**File:** `/app/papers/PMC6786370.md`\n\n**Full Content Summary:**\nA study of 634 Chinese patients with acute ischemic stroke (IS) who received antiplatelet therapy (clopidogrel or aspirin) and were followed for 1 year. The study evaluated contributions of SNPs in NR1I2 (Pregnane X receptor gene, rs13059232) and CYP2C19 alleles (*2, *3) to clopidogrel resistance (CR) and long-term clinical outcomes (Modified Rankin Scale scores and MACCE). Results showed CYP2C19*2/*3 allele carriers had significantly higher CR risk (OR 2.108, 95% CI 1.069–3.808, P=0.029). NR1I2 rs13059232 CC genotype carriers also had higher CR (OR 2.211, P=0.012). Both were independent predictors of CR. However, only NR1I2 rs13059232 — not CYP2C19*2/*3 — was an independent predictor of long-term clinical outcomes (MACCE) in clopidogrel-treated patients (P<0.001). No such effect was seen in the aspirin cohort, highlighting NR1I2 as a clopidogrel-specific biomarker.\n\n**Relevance to CYP2C19 (*1, *2, *3, *17):** Directly relevant. CYP2C19*2 and *3 are key variables:\n- CYP2C19*2 (rs4244285): genotyped; carrier status associated with clopidogrel resistance\n- CYP2C19*3 (rs4986893): genotyped; carrier status associated with clopidogrel resistance\n- Combined *2/*3 carrier group found to be an independent risk factor for CR, but not for 1-year clinical outcomes\n- CYP2C19*17 and *1 are not explicitly genotyped in this study\n\n**Drugs mentioned relating to CYP2C19:**\n- Clopidogrel (primary study drug, requiring CYP2C19-mediated activation)\n- Aspirin (comparison antiplatelet cohort)\n\n**Phenotypes/Conditions relating to CYP2C19:**\n- Clopidogrel resistance (CR) in acute ischemic stroke patients\n- MACCE (major adverse cardiovascular and cerebrovascular events)\n- Acute ischemic stroke\n- Loss-of-function metabolizer phenotype (*2/*3 carriers)\n\n---\n\n### 6. PMC6816974\n**File:** `/app/papers/PMC6816974.md`\n\n**Full Content Summary:**\nA study of 168 Chinese patients with coronary artery disease (CAD) who underwent PCI and received dual antiplatelet therapy (clopidogrel + aspirin) with 1–2 year follow-up. CYP2C19*2 (rs4244285), CYP2C19*3 (rs4986893), and ABCB1 3435CT (rs1045642) were genotyped by qRT-PCR. Thromboelastography measured platelet inhibition. Key finding: CYP2C19*3 — not CYP2C19*2 or ABCB1 — was an independent predictor of high on-treatment platelet reactivity (HPR) post-PCI (OR 22.97, 95% CI 8.309–63.502, P<0.001) and of ischemic cardiovascular adverse events (OR 8.562, P=0.046). CYP2C19*17 is mentioned in the background as a gain-of-function variant associated with ultrarapid enzymatic activity, lower platelet aggregation, and higher bleeding risk.\n\n**Relevance to CYP2C19 (*1, *2, *3, *17):** Directly and highly relevant:\n- CYP2C19*2 (rs4244285): genotyped; not significantly correlated with HPR or adverse events in this cohort\n- CYP2C19*3 (rs4986893): primary finding — independent predictor of post-PCI HPR and ischemic cardiovascular events\n- CYP2C19*17: discussed in the background as a gain-of-function allele associated with ultrarapid activity, lower platelet aggregation, and bleeding risk\n- CYP2C19*1 (wild-type G/G for both *2 and *3) used as reference\n\n**Drugs mentioned relating to CYP2C19:**\n- Clopidogrel (central drug — LoF alleles reduce its antiplatelet efficacy)\n- Aspirin (co-administered as DAPT)\n- Prasugrel and ticagrelor (mentioned as alternative P2Y12 inhibitors)\n\n**Phenotypes/Conditions relating to CYP2C19:**\n- High on-treatment platelet reactivity (HPR) post-PCI\n- Ischemic cardiovascular adverse events (stent thrombosis, recurrent unstable angina, recurrent AMI, cardiac death)\n- Coronary artery disease (CAD)\n- Poor/reduced metabolizer phenotype (CYP2C19*3 LoF carriers)\n- Ultrarapid metabolizer phenotype (CYP2C19*17 carriers — discussed, not genotyped in this study)\n\n---\n\n### 7. PMC7028104\n**File:** `/app/papers/PMC7028104.md`\n\n**Full Content Summary:**\nA pharmacokinetic study in 30 healthy Nigerian volunteers comparing the disposition of artemether-lumefantrine (80/480 mg, twice daily for 3 days) in CYP2B6*1/*1 (non-carriers, n=15) vs. CYP2B6*6/*6 (homozygous carriers, n=15). Selected from a cohort of 150 genotyped volunteers. Key findings: CYP2B6*6/*6 volunteers showed a non-significant 26% lower artemether clearance and 35% greater AUC compared to *1/*1; the artemether-to-dihydroartemisinin metabolic ratio was significantly 73% higher in *6/*6 (p=significant), confirming CYP2B6's role in artemether-to-dihydroartemisinin biotransformation. Lumefantrine and desbutyllumefantrine pharmacokinetics were unaffected by CYP2B6 genotype. The paper concludes that CYP2B6*6 reduces conversion of artemether to its active metabolite dihydroartemisinin, but the broad therapeutic index of artemether-lumefantrine likely prevents clinical impact.\n\n**Relevance to CYP2C19 (*1, *2, *3, *17):** Not relevant. This paper is focused entirely on CYP2B6*6 and its effect on antimalarial drug (artemether/lumefantrine) metabolism. CYP2C19 is not mentioned.\n\n**Drugs mentioned relating to CYP2C19:** None. The drugs studied are artemether and lumefantrine (antimalarials), whose metabolism is primarily mediated by CYP3A4/5 and CYP2B6, not CYP2C19.\n\n**Phenotypes/Conditions relating to CYP2C19:** None.\n\n---\n\n### 8. PMC8359222\n**File:** `/app/papers/PMC8359222.md`\n\n**Full Content Summary:**\nA single-arm prospective intervention trial in 60 de novo kidney transplant recipients (February 2019 – July 2020) evaluating a genotype-guided tacrolimus dosing algorithm instead of standard bodyweight-based dosing (0.2 mg/kg/day). The algorithm incorporated CYP3A4*22 and CYP3A5*1/*3 genotypes, body surface area, and age. Target tacrolimus predose concentration (C0) was 7.5–12.5 ng/mL, measured at day 3 post-transplant. Results: 60% of patients reached the target at first steady-state on day 3, compared to ~38% historically with standard dosing. Median time to first target achievement was 3 days vs. 6–7 days historically. Three patients had biopsy-proven acute rejection (5%). The algorithm significantly improved early tacrolimus exposure targeting, reducing the time outside the therapeutic window.\n\n**Relevance to CYP2C19 (*1, *2, *3, *17):** Not relevant. The paper focuses exclusively on CYP3A4*22 and CYP3A5*1/*3 genotypes and their influence on tacrolimus pharmacokinetics. CYP2C19 is not mentioned anywhere in the paper.\n\n**Drugs mentioned relating to CYP2C19:** None. The drug studied is tacrolimus (an immunosuppressant metabolized by CYP3A4 and CYP3A5, not CYP2C19).\n\n**Phenotypes/Conditions relating to CYP2C19:** None.\n\n---\n\n## Summary Table\n\n| PMC ID | Title/Topic | CYP2C19 Relevant | Alleles Discussed | Key CYP2C19 Drug | Key Conditions |\n|---|---|---|---|---|---|\n| PMC5157199 | Selumetinib in papillary thyroid cancer (BRAF focus) | No | None | None | None |\n| PMC5331771 | Clopidogrel polymorphisms and platelet reactivity in Han Chinese (1-year PCI cohort) | Yes (highly) | *2, *3, *17 | Clopidogrel | HTPR, MACE, CAD post-PCI |\n| PMC5533343 | Darapladib pharmacogenetics in STABILITY & SOLID-TIMI 52 | No | None | None | None |\n| PMC5903579 | ICPC design — clopidogrel pharmacogenomics consortium | Yes (highly) | *2, *3, *17 | Clopidogrel | HTPR, ACS, ischemic stroke, stent thrombosis |\n| PMC6786370 | NR1I2 and CYP2C19 SNPs in clopidogrel resistance (acute ischemic stroke) | Yes | *2, *3 | Clopidogrel, Aspirin | Clopidogrel resistance, MACCE, ischemic stroke |\n| PMC6816974 | CYP2C19 and ABCB1 polymorphisms in post-PCI cardiovascular events | Yes (highly) | *2, *3, *17 | Clopidogrel, Aspirin | HPR post-PCI, ischemic cardiovascular events, CAD |\n| PMC7028104 | CYP2B6*6 and artemether-lumefantrine pharmacokinetics | No | None (CYP2B6 only) | None | None |\n| PMC8359222 | CYP3A4/5-guided tacrolimus dosing in renal transplantation | No | None (CYP3A4/5 only) | None | None |\n\n**Summary of CYP2C19-relevant papers:** PMC5331771, PMC5903579, PMC6786370, and PMC6816974 are all directly relevant to CYP2C19 variants. PMC5157199\n**File:** `/app/papers/PMC5157199.md`\n\n**Full Content Summary:**\nA multicenter, open-label Phase II trial evaluating selumetinib (AZD6244/ARRY-142886), an oral MEK-1/2 inhibitor, in 39 patients with iodine-131 refractory papillary thyroid carcinoma (IRPTC). The primary endpoint (objective response rate ≥20%) was not met — only 1 partial response (3%) was achieved. Stable disease occurred in 54% of evaluable patients, with a median progression-free survival (PFS) of 32 weeks. The paper found a non-significant trend toward improved PFS in BRAF V600E mutant tumors (33 vs. 11 weeks). The study assessed BRAF, NRAS, and HRAS mutations. Common adverse events included rash, fatigue, diarrhea, and peripheral edema. The paper concluded selumetinib should not be pursued in BRAF wild-type IRPTC and that future trials should stratify by BRAF V600E status.\n\n**Relevance to CYP2C19 (*1, *2, *3, *17):** Not relevant. No mention of CYP2C19, its alleles, or its metabolic phenotypes anywhere in the paper.\n\n**Drugs mentioned relating to CYP2C19:** None. PMC5331771\n**File:** `/app/papers/PMC5331771.md`\n\n**Full Content Summary:**\nA cohort study of 336 Han Chinese patients treated with percutaneous coronary intervention (PCI) and followed for 1 year, investigating the effect of clopidogrel-related gene polymorphisms on platelet reactivity and cardiovascular clinical outcomes. Platelet reactivity was measured by the VerifyNow P2Y12 assay. CYP2C19 (*2, *3, *17), ABCB1, ITGB3, CYP2C9*3, CYP2B6*9, and P2Y12 alleles were genotyped. Key results: CYP2C19*2 and P2Y12 carriers had high on-treatment platelet reactivity (HTPR) and more adverse endpoints; CYP2C19*17 carriers showed improved (lower) platelet reactivity and fewer adverse endpoints. Carriers of CYP2C19*2 had more clinical endpoints (76.19% vs. 43.20%, P<0.001) and HTPR (194.25 vs. 151.38 PRU, P<0.001). CYP2C19*17 carriers had substantially lower platelet reactivity (97.17 vs. 169.08 PRU, P=0.003) and fewer endpoints. No cardiovascular deaths were observed in endpoint patients.\n\n**Relevance to CYP2C19 (*1, *2, *3, *17):** Directly and highly relevant. All three loss-of-function and gain-of-function variants are central study endpoints:\n- CYP2C19*2: associated with HTPR and more major adverse cardiovascular events (MACE)\n- CYP2C19*3: assessed as part of the loss-of-function panel\n- CYP2C19*17: associated with improved platelet reactivity and fewer clinical endpoints (gain-of-function)\n- CYP2C19*1 (wild type) is used implicitly as the reference group\n\n**Drugs mentioned relating to CYP2C19:**\n- Clopidogrel (primary subject of the study — a CYP2C19 prodrug requiring bioactivation)\n- Aspirin (used as part of dual antiplatelet therapy)\n\n**Phenotypes/Conditions relating to CYP2C19:**\n- High on-treatment platelet reactivity (HTPR) / clopidogrel resistance\n- Major adverse cardiovascular events (MACE) after PCI\n- Coronary artery disease\n- Poor metabolizer phenotype (CYP2C19*2 and *3 carriers)\n- Ultrarapid/enhanced metabolizer phenotype (CYP2C19*17 carriers)\n\n---\n\n### 3. PMC5533343\n**File:** `/app/papers/PMC5533343.md`\n\n**Full Content Summary:**\nA comprehensive pharmacogenetic meta-analysis of two large Phase III cardiovascular outcomes trials of darapladib — a lipoprotein-associated phospholipase A2 (Lp-PLA2) inhibitor — the STABILITY trial (stable coronary heart disease, n=15,828) and SOLID-TIMI 52 (acute coronary syndrome, n=13,026). Together, ~24,000 patients were genotyped using genome-wide arrays. The analysis investigated baseline Lp-PLA2 activity, efficacy endpoints (major coronary events, myocardial infarction), and tolerability (diarrhea, odor). Replicated six known loci (APOE, CELSR2, LPA, PLA2G7, LDLR, SCARB1) and identified three novel loci (TOMM5, FRMD5, LPL) influencing Lp-PLA2 activity. No variants associated with Lp-PLA2 activity at baseline were associated with drug response endpoints. Only one common variant showed a differential response to darapladib by treatment arm. The paper concluded that genetics did not identify meaningful responsive subgroups for darapladib. Darapladib failed its primary endpoints in both trials.\n\n**Relevance to CYP2C19 (*1, *2, *3, *17):** Not relevant. The paper includes ADME candidate gene analysis, and CYP enzymes are mentioned in the context of darapladib metabolism, but CYP2C19 specifically is not discussed, and no CYP2C19 alleles (*1, *2, *3, *17) are mentioned.\n\n**Drugs mentioned relating to CYP2C19:** None directly. PMC5903579\n**File:** `/app/papers/PMC5903579.md`\n\n**Full Content Summary:**\nA rationale and design paper for the International Clopidogrel Pharmacogenomics Consortium (ICPC), a multi-site (17 sites, 13 countries) consortium contributing individual patient data from 8,829 patients treated with clopidogrel. The goal is to perform candidate gene and genome-wide association studies (GWAS) to identify all genetic determinants of clopidogrel pharmacodynamics (platelet reactivity) and clinical response. Platelet function was assessed by VASP assay, Light Transmittance Aggregometry (LTA), and VerifyNow P2Y12 assay. A proof-of-principle analysis in 5,328 Caucasian patients confirmed that CYP2C19*2 was independently associated with higher on-treatment platelet reactivity (Beta=0.37; p=5.1×10−40). The paper provides a detailed review of CYP2C19 variants and their mechanisms, noting that CYP2C19*2 and *3 are loss-of-function (LoF) alleles reducing active metabolite levels, while CYP2C19*17 is a putative gain-of-function (GoF) allele. The paper also discusses ABCB1, CYP2B6, PON1, CES1, and B4GALT2 as other potential contributors.\n\n**Relevance to CYP2C19 (*1, *2, *3, *17):** Directly and highly relevant. CYP2C19 is the central gene of interest:\n- CYP2C19*2 (rs4244285): strongest known determinant of clopidogrel platelet reactivity, LoF allele — ~15% AF in Caucasians, ~29–35% in Asians; FDA boxed warning for *2 homozygotes (poor metabolizers)\n- CYP2C19*3 (rs4986893): LoF allele co-studied with *2\n- CYP2C19*17: putative GoF allele; linked genetically to *2 (not independently associated per Lewis et al.)\n- CYP2C19*1 implied as reference/wild-type\n\n**Drugs mentioned relating to CYP2C19:**\n- Clopidogrel (central drug — requires CYP2C19 bioactivation to active thiol metabolite for P2Y12 inhibition)\n- Prasugrel (compared, not affected by CYP2C19 polymorphisms)\n- Ticagrelor (compared, not affected by CYP2C19 polymorphisms)\n- Aspirin (co-administered)\n\n**Phenotypes/Conditions relating to CYP2C19:**\n- High on-treatment platelet reactivity (HTPR) / clopidogrel resistance\n- Poor metabolizer phenotype (CYP2C19*2 homozygotes)\n- Atherothrombotic events, stent thrombosis\n- Acute coronary syndrome (ACS)\n- Ischemic stroke\n- Post-PCI outcomes\n- Bleeding risk (CYP2C19*17 carriers)\n\n---\n\n### 5. PMC3304338\n\n**File:** `/app/papers/PMC3304338.md`\n\n**Title:** \"Influence of paraoxonase-1 Q192R and cytochrome P450 2C19 polymorphisms on clopidogrel response\"\n\n**Authors:** Kreutz RP, Nystrom P, Kreutz Y, et al.\n**Journal:** Clinical Pharmacology: Advances and Applications (2012)\n\n### Summary\nThis study examined 151 patients of mixed racial background with established coronary artery disease who received clopidogrel. PMC3760447\n\n**File:** `/app/papers/PMC3760447.md`\n\n**Title:** \"CYP2C19 polymorphisms in the Thai population and the clinical response to clopidogrel in patients with atherothrombotic-risk factors\"\n\n**Authors:** Sukasem C, Tunthong R, Chamnanphon M, et al.\n**Journal:** Pharmacogenomics and Personalized Medicine (2013)\n\n### Summary\nThis study determined the prevalence of CYP2C19 *2, *3, and *17 allele variants in 1,051 Thai patients using a microarray-based genotyping technique. PMC3773276\n\n**File:** `/app/papers/PMC3773276.md`\n\n**Title:** \"The CYP2C19*17 Variant is not Independently Associated with Clopidogrel Response\"\n\n**Authors:** Lewis JP, Stephens SH, Horenstein RB, et al.\n**Journal:** Journal of Thrombosis and Haemostasis (2013)\n\n### Summary\nThis study from the Pharmacogenomics of Anti-Platelet Intervention (PAPI) Study genotyped 621 subjects for CYP2C19 *2 and *17, then evaluated their effects — individually and jointly — on clopidogrel prodrug levels, active metabolite levels, and ADP-stimulated platelet aggregation before and after clopidogrel exposure. Key finding: CYP2C19 *2 and *17 are in linkage disequilibrium (|D'|=1.0; r2=0.07). CYP2C19 *2 was strongly associated with reduced active metabolite levels (beta=-5.24, P=3.0x10-9) and increased post-treatment platelet aggregation (beta=7.55, P=2.9x10-16) both with and without adjustment for *17. In contrast, CYP2C19 *17 was nominally associated with improved clopidogrel response before adjustment for *2 (active metabolite: beta=1.57, P=0.04; platelet aggregation: beta=-1.98, P=0.01), but this association was completely abolished after adjusting for *2 (P=0.59 and P=0.69, respectively). Stratified analyses confirmed that *2, not *17, was the primary determinant. PMC3862586\n\n**File:** `/app/papers/PMC3862586.md`\n\n**Title:** \"Cytochrome P450 3A4*22, PPAR-alpha, and ARNT polymorphisms and clopidogrel response\"\n\n**Authors:** Kreutz RP, Owens J, Jin Y, et al.\n**Journal:** Clinical Pharmacology: Advances and Applications (2013)\n\n### Summary\nThis study hypothesized that CYP3A4 phenotype-modifying variants (CYP3A4 *22, PPAR-alpha rs4253728 and rs4823613, and ARNT rs2134688) might influence clopidogrel response, since CYP3A enzymes contribute to clopidogrel active metabolite generation. 211 patients of mixed racial background with coronary artery disease receiving clopidogrel were genotyped using TaqMan assays. The methylation level correlated inversely with OCT3 expression.\n\n### CYP2C19 Relevance\n**Not relevant.** This paper focuses entirely on SLC22A3 (OCT3) — a drug transporter gene — and its epigenetic/genetic regulation. PMC3760447\n\n**File:** `/app/papers/PMC3760447.md`\n\n**Title:** \"CYP2C19 polymorphisms in the Thai population and the clinical response to clopidogrel in patients with atherothrombotic-risk factors\"\n\n**Authors:** Sukasem C, Tunthong R, Chamnanphon M, et al.\n**Journal:** Pharmacogenomics and Personalized Medicine (2013)\n\n### Summary\nThis study determined the prevalence of CYP2C19 *2, *3, and *17 allele variants in 1,051 Thai patients using a microarray-based genotyping technique. Key allele frequency findings:\n- *1/*1 (extensive metabolizer): 40.72%\n- *1/*2 (intermediate metabolizer): 35.10%\n- *1/*3 (intermediate metabolizer): 6.85%\n- *2/*2 (poor metabolizer): 7.32%\n- *2/*3 (poor metabolizer): 5.61%\n- *1/*17 (ultra-rapid metabolizer): 4.30%\n- *3/*3 (poor metabolizer): 0.10% (1 patient)\n\nOverall predicted phenotype distribution: 40.72% extensive metabolizers (EM), 41.95% intermediate metabolizers (IM), 13.03% poor metabolizers (PM), 4.30% ultra-rapid metabolizers (URM). PMC3773276\n\n**File:** `/app/papers/PMC3773276.md`\n\n**Title:** \"The CYP2C19*17 Variant is not Independently Associated with Clopidogrel Response\"\n\n**Authors:** Lewis JP, Stephens SH, Horenstein RB, et al.\n**Journal:** Journal of Thrombosis and Haemostasis (2013)\n\n### Summary\nThis study from the Pharmacogenomics of Anti-Platelet Intervention (PAPI) Study genotyped 621 subjects for CYP2C19 *2 and *17, then evaluated their effects — individually and jointly — on clopidogrel prodrug levels, active metabolite levels, and ADP-stimulated platelet aggregation before and after clopidogrel exposure. All six patients were genotyped for six CYP genes using the Affymetrix DMET 1.0 assay; CYP2C19*17 was additionally typed by PCR/RFLP. Patients were classified as extensive metabolizers (EM) or reduced metabolizers (RM). Key finding: CYP2C19 RM status was associated with significantly lower clopidogrel active metabolite exposure (p=0.0015) and higher VASP-PRI and VerifyNow PRU values (p<0.05), indicating reduced platelet inhibition. Prasugrel showed no significant pharmacokinetic or pharmacodynamic difference between EM and RM groups. CYP2C19 inhibitors (e.g., omeprazole, lansoprazole) were noted to further dampen clopidogrel response when patients taking them were grouped with RM.\n\n**CYP2C19 Relevance:** HIGHLY RELEVANT. CYP2C19 is the central gene of the study.\n- Variants explicitly studied: *1A, *2A, *8, *17 (and combinations thereof)\n- Genotypes described: *17/*17, *1A/*17, *1A/*1A (all classified as EM); *1A/*2A, *1A/*8, *2A/*2A (all classified as RM)\n- *1 is the wild-type reference allele\n- *2 is the primary loss-of-function allele driving reduced metabolizer status\n- *17 is the gain-of-function allele; *17 carriers grouped with EMs\n- *3 is not specifically genotyped in this cohort (only *2A and *8 among reduced-function alleles)\n\n**Drugs Related to CYP2C19:**\n- Clopidogrel (primary substrate — RM status impairs activation)\n- Prasugrel (comparator — not significantly affected by CYP2C19 status)\n- Omeprazole (mentioned as a CYP2C19 inhibitor used by some patients)\n- Lansoprazole (mentioned as a CYP2C19 inhibitor; discussed as potentially dampening clopidogrel response)\n- Ketoconazole (discussed in background as a CYP3A4/5 inhibitor comparator)\n- Aspirin (co-administered background therapy)\n\n**Phenotypes/Conditions Related to CYP2C19:**\n- Extensive metabolizer (EM): normal/near-normal CYP2C19 activity; adequate clopidogrel activation and platelet inhibition\n- Reduced metabolizer (RM): at least one reduced-function allele (*2 or *8); significantly lower clopidogrel active metabolite exposure, higher on-treatment platelet reactivity (higher PRI and PRU)\n- Clopidogrel resistance / poor pharmacodynamic response to clopidogrel\n- High residual platelet reactivity following clopidogrel (linked to adverse cardiovascular outcomes after stenting)\n- Clinical context: stable coronary artery disease (CAD)\n\n---\n\n## Paper 3: PMC2789741\n\n**File:** `/app/papers/PMC2789741.md`\n\n**Full Content Summary:**\n- **Title:** Influence of CYP2C9 and VKORC1 on Warfarin Response during Initiation of Therapy\n- **Authors:** Limdi, Wiener, Goldstein et al.\n- **Journal:** Blood Cells, Molecules & Diseases (2009 Mar 17)\n- **DOI:** 10.1016/j.bcmd.2009.01.019\n- **PMID:** 19297219\n- **Abstract/Content:** A prospective cohort study (Pharmacogenetic Optimization of Anticoagulation Therapy, POAT) of 521 patients (250 African Americans, 271 European Americans) on warfarin for at least 2 years. The study examined the influence of CYP2C9 and VKORC1 C1173T variants on warfarin response during the first 30 days of therapy, including rate of INR increase, anticoagulation maintenance, risk of over-anticoagulation (INR >4), dose adjustments, and proportion of time in therapeutic range. Key findings: variant VKORC1 (with or without variant CYP2C9) significantly accelerated attainment of target INR, increased dose adjustment frequency, and elevated risk of over-anticoagulation in European Americans. CYP2C9 and VKORC1 together explained 6.3% of variance in dose change over 30 days. CYP2C9 variants present in African Americans were *5, *6, *11; in European Americans *2 and *3.\n\n**CYP2C19 Relevance:** NOT RELEVANT. This paper exclusively focuses on CYP2C9 and VKORC1 in the context of warfarin pharmacogenomics. CYP2C19 is not mentioned anywhere in the paper.\n\n**Drugs Related to CYP2C19:** None. The only drug discussed is warfarin, which is metabolized by CYP2C9 (not CYP2C19).\n\n**Phenotypes/Conditions Related to CYP2C19:** None. The phenotypes discussed — over-anticoagulation, INR instability, and hemorrhage — relate to CYP2C9 and VKORC1 variation, not CYP2C19.\n\n---\n\n## Paper 4: PMC3061841\n\n**File:** `/app/papers/PMC3061841.md`\n\n**Full Content Summary:**\n- **Title:** The Relation Between CYP2C19 Genotype and Phenotype in Stented Patients on Maintenance Dual Antiplatelet Therapy\n- **Authors:** Gurbel, Shuldiner, Bliden et al.\n- **Journal:** American Heart Journal (2011 Mar)\n- **DOI:** 10.1016/j.ahj.2010.12.011\n- **PMID:** 21392617\n- **Abstract/Content:** A study of 118 stented patients on maintenance dual antiplatelet therapy (DAPT, clopidogrel + aspirin) for ≥2 weeks, plus 143 patients with stable CAD on aspirin alone. CYP2C19 *2 (rs4244285), *3 (rs4986893), and *17 (rs12248560) were genotyped by TaqMan assay. *3 was found in only one Asian subject (excluded). The study analyzed the association of *2 (loss-of-function) and *17 (gain-of-function) alleles with platelet reactivity using light transmittance aggregometry (5 and 20 uM ADP). Key findings: *2 allele carriers on DAPT had significantly higher platelet aggregation and higher prevalence of high platelet reactivity (HPR) (49% vs 26% in *1/*1, p=0.006 at 20 uM). *17 carriers showed lower HPR (20% vs 26% in *1/*1). A gradient of platelet reactivity across diplotypes was observed. Neither allele predicted platelet reactivity in aspirin-only patients. CYP2C19 genotype and HPR were found to be imperfect but complementary surrogates, both predicting cardiovascular events.\n\n**CYP2C19 Relevance:** HIGHLY RELEVANT. CYP2C19 is the central focus.\n- Variants explicitly studied: *1 (wild-type), *2 (loss-of-function), *3 (loss-of-function, genotyped but excluded from main analysis due to rarity), *17 (gain-of-function)\n- All four variants (*1, *2, *3, *17) are directly genotyped and discussed\n- Six diplotypes analyzed: *17/*17, *1/*17, *1/*1, *2/*17, *1/*2, *2/*2\n- Phenotypes classified: Extensive metabolizer (EM: *17/*17, *1/*17), Normal metabolizer (NM: *1/*1), Intermediate metabolizer (IM: *1/*2, *2/*17), Poor metabolizer (PM: *2/*2)\n\n**Drugs Related to CYP2C19:**\n- Clopidogrel (primary substrate studied; *2 allele impairs activation; the DAPT context)\n- Aspirin (co-administered in DAPT; also the comparator therapy for aspirin-alone group)\n\n**Phenotypes/Conditions Related to CYP2C19:**\n- Extensive metabolizer (EM): *17/*17 or *1/*17 diplotypes; lower platelet reactivity expected\n- Normal metabolizer (NM): *1/*1; 26% HPR prevalence\n- Intermediate metabolizer (IM): *1/*2 or *2/*17; elevated platelet reactivity\n- Poor metabolizer (PM): *2/*2; highest platelet reactivity; 49% HPR prevalence on DAPT\n- High platelet reactivity (HPR): defined by ADP-induced aggregation cutpoints; a surrogate for adverse cardiovascular outcomes\n- Stent thrombosis risk (referenced clinically)\n- Stable coronary artery disease (CAD); post-PCI (percutaneous coronary intervention) context\n\n---\n\n## Paper 5: PMC3071307\n\n**File:** `/app/papers/PMC3071307.md`\n\n**Full Content Summary:**\n- **Title:** The influence of CYP 2C19*2 polymorphism on platelet function testing during single antiplatelet treatment with clopidogrel\n- **Authors:** Pettersen, Arnesen, Opstad, Seljeflot\n- **Journal:** Thrombosis Journal (2011 Mar 22)\n- **DOI:** 10.1186/1477-9560-9-4\n- **PMID:** 21426546\n- **Abstract/Content:** A randomized study of patients with symptomatic coronary artery disease previously on chronic single aspirin therapy who were switched to clopidogrel. In 219 randomly selected clopidogrel-treated patients, platelet reactivity was evaluated by VASP-PRI and VerifyNow P2Y12 (PRU). The CYP2C19*2 G/A polymorphism (rs4244285) was genotyped. 64 patients (29%) were carriers of the *2 allele (GA or AA genotype). Carriers had significantly higher platelet reactivity: VASP-PRI 50.9% vs 38.3% (p=0.001) and PRU 165 vs 124 (p<0.001). Frequency of clopidogrel resistance was 32% in carriers vs 16% in wild-type by VASP-PRI (p=0.006), and 53% vs 22% by VerifyNow (p<0.001). The paper also compares VASP-PRI and VerifyNow as platelet function testing tools in this pharmacogenetic context.\n\n**CYP2C19 Relevance:** HIGHLY RELEVANT. CYP2C19*2 is the central genetic focus.\n- Variant explicitly studied: *2 (G681A SNP, rs4244285; described as the G/A polymorphism)\n- Wild-type comparator: *1 (GG genotype)\n- *17 and *3 are not genotyped in this study\n- Genotype groups: GG (wild-type/*1 homozygotes), GA (heterozygous *2 carriers), AA (homozygous *2 carriers); GA and AA were combined as \"carriers\"\n\n**Drugs Related to CYP2C19:**\n- Clopidogrel (the sole antiplatelet drug under study; CYP2C19*2 reduces its bioactivation)\n- Aspirin (prior/background therapy; the comparator arm of the randomized study)\n\n**Phenotypes/Conditions Related to CYP2C19:**\n- Clopidogrel resistance: defined by high VASP-PRI (>50%) and/or high PRU (VerifyNow); significantly more frequent in *2 carriers\n- Increased on-treatment platelet reactivity in *2 carriers\n- Symptomatic coronary artery disease (the clinical population studied)\n\n---\n\n## Paper 6: PMC3159941\n\n**File:** `/app/papers/PMC3159941.md`\n\n**Full Content Summary:**\n- **Title:** Cytochrome P450 2C19 Polymorphism is Associated with Reduced Clopidogrel Response in Cerebrovascular Disease\n- **Authors:** Lee, Lee, Lee\n- **Journal:** Yonsei Medical Journal (2011 Jul 18)\n- **DOI:** 10.3349/ymj.2011.52.5.734\n- **PMID:** 21786436\n- **Abstract/Content:** A retrospective study of 166 patients with cerebrovascular disease (142 cerebral infarction, 24 intra/extracranial artery stenosis) who received clopidogrel. CYP2C19 genotyping was performed using the Seeplex CYP2C19 ACE Genotyping system, which detects *2 and *3 alleles. Genotype groups and their frequencies: *1/*1 (EM): 68 (40.9%); *1/*2: 56 (33.7%); *1/*3: 18 (10.8%); *2/*2: 14 (8.4%); *2/*3: 9 (5.4%); *3/*3: 1 (0.6%). Classified as extensive (EM: *1/*1), intermediate (IM: *1/*2 or *1/*3), or poor (PM: *2/*2, *2/*3, *3/*3) metabolizers. Clopidogrel resistance assessed by VerifyNow P2Y12 (PRU and % inhibition). PRU was significantly higher from EM to IM to PM (195.0, 237.9, 302.2; p<0.001). % inhibition was significantly lower from EM to IM to PM (44.6%, 30.5%, 14.0%; p<0.001). Subjects received prasugrel 10 mg or clopidogrel 75 mg for 10 days (with 14-day washout and crossover). CYP2C19 genotypes classified into: Rapid Metabolizers (RM: *1/*1), Intermediate Metabolizers (IM: *1/*2 or *1/*3), and Poor Metabolizers (PM: *2/*2 or *2/*3). 83 subjects completed both periods. Key findings: Pras-AM (prasugrel active metabolite) exposure was similar across RM, IM, and PM groups; Clop-AM (clopidogrel active metabolite) exposure was significantly lower in IMs vs RMs (90% CI 0.62–0.83) and in PMs vs IMs (90% CI 0.53–0.82). Inhibition of platelet aggregation (IPA) was consistent across groups for prasugrel (80.2%, 84.2%, 80.2% for RM, IM, PM respectively) but significantly different for clopidogrel (59.7%, 56.2%, 36.8%; p<0.001). This study specifically examined the high-frequency CYP2C19 poor metabolizer population in Chinese subjects.\n\n**CYP2C19 Relevance:** HIGHLY RELEVANT. CYP2C19 variants are the defining stratification variable of the entire study.\n- Variants explicitly studied: *1 (wild-type), *2 (loss-of-function), *3 (loss-of-function)\n- Genotype groups: *1/*1 (RM), *1/*2 (IM), *1/*3 (IM), *2/*2 (PM), *2/*3 (PM)\n- *17 is not studied in this population (not noted as relevant in Chinese subjects)\n- The paper uses \"Rapid Metabolizer\" rather than \"Extensive Metabolizer\" for *1/*1\n\n**Drugs Related to CYP2C19:**\n- Clopidogrel (active metabolite exposure and IPA significantly affected by *2 and *3 alleles)\n- Prasugrel (comparator; active metabolite exposure and IPA largely unaffected by CYP2C19 status)\n\n**Phenotypes/Conditions Related to CYP2C19:**\n- Rapid Metabolizer (RM: *1/*1): normal clopidogrel and prasugrel metabolism; adequate platelet inhibition\n- Intermediate Metabolizer (IM: *1/*2, *1/*3): reduced clopidogrel active metabolite exposure and modestly reduced IPA\n- Poor Metabolizer (PM: *2/*2, *2/*3): markedly reduced clopidogrel active metabolite exposure; IPA only 36.8% vs 59.7% in RM (p<0.001); clinically significant clopidogrel resistance\n- Reduced platelet inhibition / clopidogrel non-response in IM and PM\n- Study population: healthy Chinese subjects (high-frequency PM population, ~15–20% PM in East Asians vs ~2–5% in Caucasians)\n\n---\n\n## Paper 8: PMC3250350\n\n**File:** `/app/papers/PMC3250350.md`\n\n**Full Content Summary:**\n- **Title:** Paraoxonase 1 (PON1) Gene Variants Are Not Associated With Clopidogrel Response\n- **Authors:** Lewis, Fisch, Ryan et al.\n- **Journal:** Clinical Pharmacology and Therapeutics (2011 Aug 31)\n- **DOI:** 10.1038/clpt.2011.194\n- **PMID:** 21881565\n- **Abstract/Content:** A study refuting a prior claim that PON1 Q192R is a major determinant of clopidogrel response. The PON1 Q192R variant was genotyped in 566 participants of the Amish Pharmacogenomics of Anti-Platelet Intervention (PAPI) study and 227 PCI patients. PON1 Q192R showed no association with pre- or post-clopidogrel platelet aggregation in either cohort, and no association with HPR or cardiovascular events. In contrast, CYP2C19*2 (rs4244285) showed a strong association with post-clopidogrel ADP-stimulated platelet aggregation (p=5.6×10^-18) and HPR (p=1.67×10^-9) in the PAPI cohort. CYP2C19*2 was also associated with cardiovascular outcomes in the PCI cohort. 49 additional PON1 SNPs spanning the locus were also evaluated — none were associated with clopidogrel response. The study also notes that CYP2C19*2 contributes ~12% of interindividual variability in clopidogrel response.\n\n**CYP2C19 Relevance:** RELEVANT (secondary but explicit and quantitatively important). CYP2C19*2 is used as a positive control and a well-validated comparator throughout the paper.\n- Variant explicitly studied: *2 (rs4244285; loss-of-function)\n- *1, *3, and *17 are not explicitly genotyped or analyzed, but *1 is the implicit wild-type reference\n- CYP2C19*2 is stated to contribute ~12% of interindividual variability in clopidogrel response\n- CYP2C19*2 genotype is shown to strongly predict post-clopidogrel platelet aggregation and HPR in the same cohorts where PON1 Q192R does not\n\n**Drugs Related to CYP2C19:**\n- Clopidogrel (CYP2C19*2 is the established predictor of variable response; used as positive control comparison throughout)\n\n**Phenotypes/Conditions Related to CYP2C19:**\n- CYP2C19*2 loss-of-function: strongly associated with increased post-clopidogrel ADP-stimulated platelet aggregation (p=5.6×10^-18) and high platelet reactivity (HPR) (p=1.67×10^-9)\n- CYP2C19*2 associated with adverse cardiovascular outcomes in PCI patients (referenced from prior work)\n- High platelet reactivity (HPR) after clopidogrel treatment\n- Coronary artery disease / percutaneous coronary intervention (PCI) patient population\n- Stent thrombosis risk (referenced in context of PON1 vs CYP2C19*2 comparison)\n\n---\n\n## Summary Table\n\n| PMCID | Relevant to CYP2C19 (*1/*2/*3/*17)? high risk of atherothrombosis and has become a cornerstone in treatment of coronary artery disease (CAD) [[1](#B1)]. {"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01WmrHAqkQwNAyv3nZ3upYrS","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→# Cytochrome P450 3A4*22, PPAR-α, and ARNT polymorphisms and clopidogrel response\n     2→\n     3→## Metadata\n     4→**Authors:** Rolf P Kreutz, Janelle Owens, Yan Jin, Perry Nystrom, Zeruesenay Desta, Yvonne Kreutz, Jeffrey A Breall, Lang Li, ChienWei Chiang, Richard J Kovacs, David A Flockhart\n     5→**Journal:** Clinical Pharmacology : Advances and Applications\n     6→**Date:** 2013 Dec 9\n     7→**DOI:** [10.2147/CPAA.S53151](https://doi.org/10.2147/CPAA.S53151)\n     8→**PMID:** 24353446\n     9→**PMCID:** PMC3862586\n    10→**URL:** https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3862586/\n    11→**PDF:** [https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC3862586/pdf/cpaa-5-185.pdf](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC3862586/pdf/cpaa-5-185.pdf)\n    12→\n    13→## Abstract\n    14→\n    15→Recent candidate gene studies using a human liver bank and in vivo validation in healthy volunteers identified polymorphisms in cytochrome P450 (CYP) 3A4 gene (CYP3A4*22), Ah-receptor nuclear translocator (ARNT), and peroxisome proliferator-activated receptor-α (PPAR-α) genes that are associated with the CYP3A4 phenotype. We hypothesized that the variants identified in these genes may be associated with altered clopidogrel response, since generation of clopidogrel active metabolite is, partially mediated by CYP3A activity. Blood samples from 211 subjects, of mixed racial background, with established coronary artery disease, who had received clopidogrel, were analyzed. Platelet aggregation was determined using light transmittance aggregometry (LTA). Genotyping for CYP2C19*2, CYP3A4*22, PPAR-α (rs4253728, rs4823613), and ARNT (rs2134688) variant alleles was performed using Taqman® assays. CYP2C19*2 genotype was associated with increased on-treatment platelet aggregation (adenosine diphosphate 20 μM; P=0.025). No significant difference in on-treatment platelet aggregation, as measured by LTA during therapy with clopidogrel, was demonstrated among the different genotypes of CYP3A4*22, PPAR-α, and ARNT. These findings suggest that clopidogrel platelet inhibition is not influenced by the genetic variants that have previously been associated with reduced CYP3A4 activity.\n    16→\n    17→Keywords: clopidogrel, pharmacogenetics, CYP450, platelet aggregation\n    18→\n    19→## Introduction\n    20→\n    21→Clopidogrel is a thienopyridine antiplatelet agent that bioactivates in a two-step process, with varying contributions to these steps from cytochrome P450 (CYP) 2C19, CYP1A2, CYP2C9, CYP3A4, CYP3A5, and CYP2B6 isoenzymes.[1](#b1-cpaa-5-185) Inactivation of the precursor, and metabolites, is dependent, predominantly, on hepatic carboxylesterase-1 enzyme.[2](#b2-cpaa-5-185),[3](#b3-cpaa-5-185) Diminished metabolic status of CYP2C19, due to drug interactions or variants in the *CYP2C19* gene, results in lower active clopidogrel metabolite generation, and higher on-treatment platelet reactivity.[4](#b4-cpaa-5-185)–[7](#b7-cpaa-5-185) However, CYP2C19 metabolic status alone was found to account for only 12% of the variability in clopidogrel response.[8](#b8-cpaa-5-185) Several lines of evidence suggest that the CYP3A4 and CYP3A5 enzymes may play a role in clopidogrel activation, although data are not uniform.[1](#b1-cpaa-5-185),[5](#b5-cpaa-5-185),[9](#b9-cpaa-5-185),[10](#b10-cpaa-5-185)*CYP3A5*3* polymorphism has not been shown to significantly predict clopidogrel pharmacokinetics.[8](#b8-cpaa-5-185),[11](#b11-cpaa-5-185),[12](#b12-cpaa-5-185)\n    22→\n    23→In humans, CYP3A isoenzymes comprise the majority of hepatic CYP450 proteins. CYP3A4 is the most abundant, and metabolizes approximately 50% of drugs which are cleared via metabolism.[13](#b13-cpaa-5-185),[14](#b14-cpaa-5-185) A high intersubject variability has been documented in the activity of CYP3A4, an enzyme that metabolizes a wide range of clinically important substrates.[14](#b14-cpaa-5-185)–[16](#b16-cpaa-5-185) Despite estimates that suggested that up to 90% of functional CYP3A4 variability is heritable, the genetic basis of CYP3A4 variable expression and activity is poorly understood.[17](#b17-cpaa-5-185) While functional variants of many CYP genes have been established, the few common exonic variants of the *CYP3A4* gene that have been reported so far only marginally explain variations in CYP3A4 activity.\n    24→\n    25→Recently, Klein et al, using a candidate gene approach, in a human liver bank, identified several single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) that are associated with CYP3A4 activity phenotype.[15](#b15-cpaa-5-185) In that study, 40 candidate genes, including 334 SNPs, were examined for association with CYP3A4 protein expression and CYP3A4 activity, measured by atorvastatin 2-hydroxylation in hepatocyte supernatants from 159 liver samples. Validation was performed by measurement of atorvastatin hydroxylation in 56 healthy volunteers, after single-dose atorvastatin administration.[15](#b15-cpaa-5-185) Among the SNPs identified, *CYP3A4*22* is a recently-identified intronic variant associated with reduced CYP3A4 activity.[14](#b14-cpaa-5-185),[16](#b16-cpaa-5-185),[18](#b18-cpaa-5-185)–[20](#b20-cpaa-5-185) The *CYP3A4*22* allele is characterized by a C>T substitution ([NG_008421.1](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/NG_008421.1):g.20493C>T), and reduced CYP3A4 messenger (m)RNA expression has been linked to the minor T-allele.[14](#b14-cpaa-5-185) Other variants newly identified are located in the peroxisome proliferator-activated receptor-α (*PPAR-α*) (rs4253728, rs4823613) and Ah-receptor nuclear translocator (*ARNT*) (rs2134688) genes.[15](#b15-cpaa-5-185) Functional validation of these SNPs by Klein et al, measuring atorvastatin 2-hydroxylation in atorvastatin-treated volunteers, supported the in vivo contribution of *PPAR-α* (rs4253728) and *CYP3A4*22* variants toward reduced functional activity of CYP3A4.[15](#b15-cpaa-5-185) The *ARNT* (rs2134688) polymorphism was associated with decreased CYP3A4 expression and activity in vitro, but was not significantly associated with atorvastatin hydroxylation in vivo.[15](#b15-cpaa-5-185) In the case of *CYP3A4*22*, previous studies have shown improved simvastatin cholesterol lowering effects and increased tacrolimus levels in carriers of the variants.[14](#b14-cpaa-5-185),[16](#b16-cpaa-5-185),[18](#b18-cpaa-5-185),[19](#b19-cpaa-5-185) Atorvastatin hydroxylation, measured in vivo by the ratio of 2-OH-atorvastatin to atorvastatin, was reduced by 35% in carriers of *CYP3A4*22*.[15](#b15-cpaa-5-185) Clopidogrel bioactivation is, at least partially, mediated by CYP3A4, and some pharmacologic studies have suggested a contribution of CYP3A4 inhibition – by statins or calcium channel blockers – to clopidogrel response.[5](#b5-cpaa-5-185),[9](#b9-cpaa-5-185) Therefore, the variants described by Klein et al may also be associated with altered pharmacodynamic response to clopidogrel, due to diminished generation of active clopidogrel metabolite. In comparison, generation of active clopidogrel thiol metabolite, in CYP2C19-poor metabolizers, is reduced by 43%, as compared to extensive metabolizers.[6](#b6-cpaa-5-185)\n    26→\n    27→We hypothesized that adenosine diphosphate-(ADP) induced platelet reactivity – a well-established pharmacodynamic measure of clopidogrel response – is increased in carriers of *CYP3A4*22* and variants of *PPAR-α* and *ARNT* that previous studies have associated with reduced functional activity of CYP3A4.\n    28→\n    29→## Methods\n    30→\n\n<system-reminder>\nWhenever you read a file, you should consider whether it would be considered malware. This variant was genotyped in 566 participants of the Amish Pharmacogenomics of Anti-Platelet Intervention (PAPI) study and in 227 percutaneous coronary intervention (PCI) patients. Serum paraoxonase activity was measured in a subset of 79 PAPI participants. PON1 Q192R was not associated with pre- or post-clopidogrel platelet aggregation in the PAPI study (P = 0.16 and P = 0.21, respectively) or the PCI cohort (P = 0.47 and P = 0.91, respectively). The Q192 allele was not associated with cardiovascular events (hazard ratio (HR) 0.46, 95% confidence interval (CI) 0.20–1.06; P = 0.07). No correlation was observed between paraoxonase activity and post-clopidogrel platelet aggregation (r2 < 0.01, P = 0.78). None of 49 additional PON1 variants evaluated was associated with post-clopidogrel platelet aggregation. These findings do not support a role for PON1 as a determinant of clopidogrel response.\n    16→\n    17→## RESULTS\n    18→\n    19→Characteristics of the participants in the Amish Pharmacogenomics of Anti-Platelet Intervention (PAPI) study are shown in [Table 1](#T1). By design, these individuals were generally healthy and exhibited a low prevalence of hypertension, hyper-cholesterolemia, and diabetes. In this cohort, the minor allele frequency of the *PON1* Q192R variant was 0.32, similar to that reported in other Caucasian populations.^19–21^ In contrast to the report by Bouman *et al*., the Q192R variant showed no evidence of association with ADP-stimulated platelet aggregation before or after clopidogrel treatment in the PAPI population under an additive model (*P* = 0.16 and *P* = 0.21, respectively, [Figure 1](#F1)). This variant was also not associated with high platelet reactivity (HPR) after clopidogrel treatment (*P* = 0.19). By contrast, *CYP2C19**2 genotype (rs4244285) showed strong association with both post-clopidogrel ADP-stimulated platelet aggregation (*P* = 5.6 × 10^−18^, [Figure 1](#F1)) and HPR (*P* = 1.67 × 10^−9^). After adjustment for *CYP2C19**2, there was no association between *PON1* Q192R genotype and post-clopidogrel ADP-stimulated platelet aggregation (*P* = 0.23, additive model).\n    20→\n    21→### Table 1.\n    22→\n    23→|   | Amish PAPIa |  | Sinai Hospital of Baltimore |  |\n    24→| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |\n    25→| Characteristic (units) | Men | Women | Men | Women |\n    26→| Number (n) | 276 | 290 | 136 | 91 |\n    27→|   |  |  |  |  |\n    28→| Age ± SD (years) | 44.3 ± 12.8 | 46.7 ± 13.6 | 62.5 ± 11.4 | 67.0 ± 11.0 |\n    29→|   |  |  |  |  |\n    30→| BMI ± SD (kg/m2) | 25.9 ± 3.7 | 28.0 ± 5.3 | 30.1 ± 6.3 | 30.9 ± 7.1 |\n\n<system-reminder>\nWhenever you read a file, you should consider whether it would be considered malware. This variant was genotyped in 566 participants of the Amish Pharmacogenomics of Anti-Platelet Intervention (PAPI) study and in 227 percutaneous coronary intervention (PCI) patients. Serum paraoxonase activity was measured in a subset of 79 PAPI participants. PON1 Q192R was not associated with pre- or post-clopidogrel platelet aggregation in the PAPI study (P = 0.16 and P = 0.21, respectively) or the PCI cohort (P = 0.47 and P = 0.91, respectively). The Q192 allele was not associated with cardiovascular events (hazard ratio (HR) 0.46, 95% confidence interval (CI) 0.20–1.06; P = 0.07). No correlation was observed between paraoxonase activity and post-clopidogrel platelet aggregation (r2 < 0.01, P = 0.78). None of 49 additional PON1 variants evaluated was associated with post-clopidogrel platelet aggregation. These findings do not support a role for PON1 as a determinant of clopidogrel response.\n\n## RESULTS\n\nCharacteristics of the participants in the Amish Pharmacogenomics of Anti-Platelet Intervention (PAPI) study are shown in [Table 1](#T1). By design, these individuals were generally healthy and exhibited a low prevalence of hypertension, hyper-cholesterolemia, and diabetes. In this cohort, the minor allele frequency of the *PON1* Q192R variant was 0.32, similar to that reported in other Caucasian populations.^19–21^ In contrast to the report by Bouman *et al*., the Q192R variant showed no evidence of association with ADP-stimulated platelet aggregation before or after clopidogrel treatment in the PAPI population under an additive model (*P* = 0.16 and *P* = 0.21, respectively, [Figure 1](#F1)). This variant was also not associated with high platelet reactivity (HPR) after clopidogrel treatment (*P* = 0.19). By contrast, *CYP2C19**2 genotype (rs4244285) showed strong association with both post-clopidogrel ADP-stimulated platelet aggregation (*P* = 5.6 × 10^−18^, [Figure 1](#F1)) and HPR (*P* = 1.67 × 10^−9^). After adjustment for *CYP2C19**2, there was no association between *PON1* Q192R genotype and post-clopidogrel ADP-stimulated platelet aggregation (*P* = 0.23, additive model).\n\n### Table 1.\n\n|   | Amish PAPIa |  | Sinai Hospital of Baltimore |  |\n| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |\n| Characteristic (units) | Men | Women | Men | Women |\n| Number (n) | 276 | 290 | 136 | 91 |\n|   |  |  |  |  |\n| Age ± SD (years) | 44.3 ± 12.8 | 46.7 ± 13.6 | 62.5 ± 11.4 | 67.0 ± 11.0 |\n|   |  |  |  |  |\n| BMI ± SD (kg/m2) | 25.9 ± 3.7 | 28.0 ± 5.3 | 30.1 ± 6.3 | 30.9 ± 7.1 |","numLines":30,"startLine":1,"totalLines":233}}}
